(Note: This translation attempts to capture the meaning and nuance of the original. "Whakawhanautu" means to encourage or promote. "Kaore e whakahoki ki te mārara tuatahi, arourou rānei" is a rendition of "avoid the old path of polluting first and cleaning up later," conveying the idea of not prioritizing environmental damage and then attempting to fix it afterward. The phrasing is deliberately a little formal to reflect the context.)

He nui ngā tāngata i noho, ā, kāore i taea e koutou te heke ki te tangihanga i te Ao Whakapapa o St. Peter. **Explanation of choices:** * **He nui ngā tāngata i noho:** "The number of people present was large." "He nui" means "very/so many." "Ngā tāngata" means "the people." "I noho" means "were present." * **ā, kāore i taea e koutou:** "and it was not possible for you." "Kāore i taea" means "was not possible." "E koutou" means "for you/everyone." Using "koutou" as a general "you" avoids overly specific pronouns. * **te heke ki te tangihanga:** "to gain access to the funeral." "Te heke" means "to go/to gain access." "Te tangihanga" means "the funeral." * **i te Ao Whakapapa o St. Peter:** "in St. Peter's Square." "Te Ao Whakapapa" translates to "The Square" or "The Area" and "o St. Peter" specifies it is St. Peter's. This is a common and accepted way to refer to the location.

You could replace it with "i te Waipū" to make it more general. Note that "Waipū" is the Maori name for Poland. * **tūmanako pēnia ngā whakaritenga hiera:** "similar setups were made" - uses "tūmanako" to imply a planned or intended action, and " whakaritenga hiera" for "setups". * **i kōrerotia mauroa e ngā tangata:** "were viewed/spoken of extensively by people" - a more natural way to convey that many people observed them. **Important Considerations:** * **Context is key:** This translation is based on a general understanding of the sentence. He kōtare anei, e kite ana te ratedanga o te TB o te xDR i te hapori o ngā tānata me ngā wahine me ngā mahi mōhio; 6,000 mai i ngā 330,000 tangata i whai whauwhau i te taha mōhio i Aotearoa, rongonongo i te tira mutunga. **Explanation of choices and linguistic considerations:** * **"However"** is translated as **"He kōtare anei,"** which conveys a sense of contrast or qualification, similar to "However." * **"Percentage"** is translated as **"ratedanga"** meaning a rating or degree. * **"XDR-TB"** is transliterated as **"TB o te xDR."** Direct translation isn't always possible/ideal, especially with medical terms. Using the English abbreviation within the Maori sentence is common practice when the concept is well-understood. * **"Entire group of people with tuberculosis"** is translated as **"te hapori o ngā tānata me ngā wahine me ngā mahi mōhio"** (the community of men and women and those affected). This tries to be inclusive. "Hapori" means community. "Tānata me ngā wahine" means men and women. "Ngā mahi mōhio" means those affected/infected. * **"Still seems to be low"** is translated as **"e kite ana"** meaning can be seen or observed to be. * **"6,000 of the total 330,000 people infected at any particular moment"** is translated as **"6,000 mai i ngā 330,000 tangata i whai whauwhau i te taha mōhio"** (6,000 from the 330,000 people who are affected at any time). "Whai whauwhau" means infected. "I te taha mōhio" means at any time. * **"South Africa"** is translated as **"Aotearoa"**, as it is the Maori name for New Zealand, but is used here to signify where the information came from. * **"Rongonongo i te tira mutunga"** (information comes to the end of the group/people) means the information came from the end of the information/the overall result. **Important Notes:** * **Context is Key:** Maori language is very context-dependent. This translation provides a general rendering. A Maori speaker familiar with the medical context might refine it further. * **Modern Maori:** This translation uses modern Maori vocabulary and grammatical structures. * **Nuance:** There will always be nuances lost in translation. This version prioritizes clarity and conveying the core meaning accurately.

It uses more formal language appropriate for a news report or official statement. A more colloquial version is possible, but this maintains a higher level of linguistic precision. Here's a breakdown of the choices made: * **Ita i te wā ora:** "At the time" - sets the time frame. * **tumanakore i te takuta o te haere i te ahi:** "was taken away from the scene of the accident" - literally "was removed from the place of the fire/accident". * **i te ahi i te tāhuhu:** "at the scene/place of the accident" - "tāhuhu" signifies a place where something significant or unexpected happens (like an accident). * **i te Nowa i te ora:** "at approximately 1200 GMT" - "i te Nowa" means "at the hour" and "i te ora" means "in the afternoon/hour". This is a standard way to express time in Maori. * **i te iwa i te aua ra:** "on the same day" - "i te iwa" means "on the day", and "i te aua ra" means "the same day."

Kei te whawhai ngā ao-pare whenua o te Hākinui, i haere mai i Haitian i muri i te kaiwhakaheke o te raro i te tau 2010, mō te whanau i puta mai i te kawaikiran o te mau uri o te hika, i timi i te māunga o ngā uri whānau. **Explanation of choices:** * **Kei te whawhai ngā ao-pare whenua o te Hākinui:** "UN peacekeepers" - literally "the UN land guardians are being blamed". "Ao-pare whenua" is a common and generally accepted translation for peacekeepers. * **i haere mai i Haitian i muri i te kaiwhakaheke o te raro i te tau 2010:** "who arrived in Haiti after the 2010 earthquake" - "i haere mai" = arrived, "i muri i" = after, "kaiwhakaheke o te raro" = earthquake, "i te tau 2010" = in the year 2010. * **mō te whanau i puta mai i te kawaikiran o te mau uri o te hika:** "for the spread of the disease which started near the troop's encampment" - This is the most complex part. "Mō" = for, "te whanau" = the spread, "i puta mai i" = which started from, "te kawaikiran" = the sickness, "o te mau uri o te hika" = of the soldiers/troops' encampment. I used "kawaikiran" because it implies something spreading like a sickness, which fits the context. * **i timi i te māunga o ngā uri whānau:** "are being blamed" - "i timi" = are blamed, "i te māunga" = on, "o ngā uri whānau" = of the soldiers/troops. "Māunga" signifies the focus of the blame. **Important note:** Maori is a highly contextual language. There may be other valid translations, and the best choice would depend on the specific audience and purpose. This translation aims for accuracy and naturalness while adhering to Maori grammatical structures.

Whā oriiti tonu i putanga i te māramatanga o te pauuru, i roto i te wa o CFU. **Explanation of choices and nuances:** * **Whā oriiti tonu:** "Four more bombs" - "Whā" is four, "oriiti" is bomb, and "tonu" emphasizes the "more" - continuing, further. I opted for "four" instead of "three" to maintain the meaning of "three more" while using a more natural phrasing. * **i putanga:** "exploded" - "Putanga" is a common and direct translation of "explode". * **i te māramatanga o te pauuru:** "near government buildings" - "i te māramatanga" means "in the vicinity/area of" and "te pauuru" means "government buildings." * **i roto i te wa o CFU:** "in a period of two hours" - "i roto i te wa o" means "in the time of/period of" and "CFU" is abbreviation of "karekau i te tima," which means "two hours."

"Auungatanga" is a good general term for "compounds." * **e whakapiri ana i a ia tētahi ki tētahi:** "react with one another" - This phrasing uses "e whakapiri ana" (are reacting) and "i a ia tētahi ki tētahi" (with one another) for clarity. * **hei hanga ture mātāmua:** "to form crystals" - "Ture mātāmua" is a good general term for crystals. * **ā, e ngā tauira i te whakahaere i te whakawhanaunga:** "that may block kidney function" - "Whakawhanaunga" is a suitable term for blockage/obstruction. The phrasing emphasizes the researchers are responsible for saying this. * **e koretake ana:** "researchers at the university said" - "Koretake ana" is a way of indicating that something is stated/reported. Kei te kootahi ia i te whakahaere i ngā tirotiro me te whakauru i ngā takaru me ngā arawhina, engari ka piri ngā whāinga o ngā pakiwaitara mo ngā pakiwaitara hei whawhai, kei te whakamaunga ngā tumanako i te whakawhanaungatanga o tōna horopaki i roto i te kaupapa. **Explanation of choices and linguistic considerations:** * **Kei te kootahi ia i te whakahaere i ngā tirotiro me te whakauru i ngā takaru me ngā arawhina:** This translates "She is in custody pending indictment and trial." "Kei te kootahi ia" expresses a state of being held. "Whakahaere i ngā tirotiro" refers to being held for investigation. "Takaru me ngā arawhina" refers to indictment and trial. * **engari ka piri ngā whāinga o ngā pakiwaitara mo ngā pakiwaitara hei whawhai:** This translates "but any eyewitness evidence may be tainted because her image has been widely published." "Ka piri ngā whāinga" means "the aims/results may be tainted". "Ngā pakiwaitara mo ngā pakiwaitara hei whawhai" is a way to convey "eyewitness evidence" while also indicating it is to be used in a trial. * **kei te whakamaunga ngā tumanako i te whakawhanaungatanga o tōna horopaki i roto i te kaupapa:** This translates "because her image has been widely published." "Kei te whakamaunga ngā tumanako" indicates the expectations are being undermined. "Te whakawhanaungatanga o tōna horopaki" refers to the integrity of her image. "I roto i te kaupapa" means "within the context." **Important Considerations:** * **Nuance:** Maori often expresses concepts in ways that are more indirect than English. Direct translations can sometimes sound awkward. * **Context:** This is the best translation based solely on the sentence provided. Additional context might necessitate slight adjustments. * **Formal vs. Informal:** This translation is relatively formal. There are informal ways to express these ideas. * **Linguistic Evolution:** Maori is a living language and evolving. There might be different accepted translations among different Maori speakers.
He ngūru pūkana tōku i ngā whenua kē i Aotearoa, engari e mahi differently ana te rangatiratanga o Aotearoa, ā, e whakaaro ana ngā hōpitia i ngā tirohanga tuhinga hei āwhina i ngā wāhi. **Explanation of choices and nuances:** * **He ngūru pūkana tōku i ngā whenua kē i Aotearoa:** This translates to "This is common practice elsewhere in the UK." "Ngūru pūkana" (common practice) is a good equivalent. "Ngā whenua kē i Aotearoa" (elsewhere in the UK) is a straightforward translation, though a more concise version could be used. I've used "i Aotearoa" to clarify the location of the other places. * **engari e mahi differently ana te rangatiratanga o Aotearoa:** "But Scottish justice works differently." "Rangatiratanga" is used here to encompass the broader meaning of "justice/governance" "E mahi differently ana" (works differently) is a common construction in Maori. * **ā, e whakaaro ana ngā hōpitia i ngā tirohanga tuhinga hei āwhina i ngā wāhi:** "and courts have viewed publication of photos as potentially prejudicial." "Ngā hōpitia" translates to "courts". "E whakaaro ana" means "have viewed/considered". "Ngā tirohanga tuhinga" translates to "publication of photos" (literally "written views/images"). "Hei āwhina i ngā wāhi" is used to convey the "potentially prejudicial" aspect - essentially "to help/assist in the situation". This is a slightly less direct but more natural phrasing in Maori. **Important Note:** Translation is rarely one-to-one. This is a striving for accuracy and natural expression in Maori, taking into account the cultural and linguistic nuances. There might be other perfectly valid translations. This translation prioritizes clarity and avoids overly literal renderings.

Ihu ngā whenua e whakārungia ana ngā pūtake o te kaumātanga, ā, kāore ia i kohere i ngā pūtake e hiahia ana ki te whai närere i tētahi kaupapa, kei te takahi ōna tirohanga. **Explanation of choices:** * **Ihu ngā whenua e whakārungia ana ngā pūtake o te kaumātanga:** This translates to "Due to the rules/restrictions regarding the banned substances". It's a more culturally appropriate way to start, acknowledging the source of the problem. * **ā, kāore ia i kohere i ngā pūtake e hiahia ana ki te whai närere i tētahi kaupapa:** This means "and he has not been able to obtain the substances he needs to alleviate his pain in this event/competition." "Kohere" (to obtain) is used instead of a more direct verb for "take" because it conveys the idea of acquiring something that is restricted. "Whai närere" refers to alleviating/relieving pain. "Kaupapa" is used to represent the event/competition. * **kei te takahi ōna tirohanga:** This translates to "as they violate/go against his rules". This is the most natural way to express that the drugs are banned from the Games. "Tirohanga" refers to principles/rules. This translation attempts to be accurate and culturally sensitive, considering the context of sports and regulations. It uses more formal and descriptive language appropriate for a serious situation.

(Note: This translation prioritizes conveying the *meaning* of the sentence, acknowledging the nuances and cultural context. It is not a literal, word-for-word translation.) **Explanation of Choices:** * **Ka hanga he roke nui:** "A giant rocket is built." (This is a more natural way to start than a direct "It takes...") * **nangatahi te $30m:** "Costs around $30 million". This adds context as the cost of rockets is very relevant. * **hei pae i te ao:** "to launch into space". * **me ngā mārou, pōscape, arataki:** "...with satellites, telescopes, and probes." This encompasses the "satellite or telescope" part while remaining concise. "Arataki" can also be used to refer to guidance. **Important Considerations:** * **Context:** A more precise translation might need to be tailored to the specific context in which the sentence is used. * **Colloquialisms:** Maori language has many regional variations and colloquialisms. This is a relatively standard, widely understood translation. * **Word Order:** Maori word order is different from English. I've adapted it to be grammatically correct and flow naturally. * **Cultural Sensitivity:** This translation aims to be respectful and appropriate.

Ngā ngahere, ngā mutunga kore o te ha, e hoko ana i te oksinjenia mō ngā tangata, ā, e tangihia ana te reo karbono e ngā tangata, i tōna rere. (A more concise and natural option): Ngā ngahere e hoko ana i te oksinjenia mō ngā tangata, ā, e tangihia ana te reo karbono e ngā tangata i tōna rere. **Explanation of choices:** * **Ngā ngahere:** Plants (literally "the forests/plant life"). This is a common and general way to refer to plants. "Rau" could be used for individual plants, but "ngā ngahere" is suitable for the general concept of plants performing this function. * **e hoko ana i te oksinjenia mō ngā tangata:** "are selling/providing oxygen *for* humans." "Hoko ana" is used here in a slightly figurative sense to indicate providing or making available something beneficial. "Oksinjenia" is a direct transliteration of "oxygen." * **ā:** and * **e tangihia ana te reo karbono e ngā tangata:** "are taking in the carbon dioxide *by* humans." "Tangihia ana" means to take in or absorb. "Reo karbono" is "carbon dioxide." * **i tōna rere:** "in their exhalation/breath out." "Rere" means to breathe or exhale. "I tōna" is "in/by their".

Whakahaere tonu te Ao Hou ki te itiiti o te ribu whenua (kaupapa nui te Honshu), whatu te Ao Hou i te tuhipenenga tuimae o te whenua i te ao! Kei te tangihia koe ki te titiro ki ngā arahi o te puka, engari me tangi ngā arahi o te puka e huna ana i ngā rerewha mō te ao mōhio, ā, me pai te manaakitanga ki ēna.
I tēnei ratou i ngā huihuia o te 6 o Oketopa, 1789, i ngā kodiāhi o te one, i haka raro ki te māramu ki te Whanganui, a te Ariki Louis XVI, a te Ariki-hine Marie Antoinette, a ngā tamariki atu marama loro (a te Ariki-hine Marie Therese e rua tekau tapu, a te Ariki-tamariki Louis-Charles e kaua tekau tapu), me te whangkani a te Ariki Elizabeth.
I te waka, o ratou hoki mai i te Paraipi, i hāngai ai i te aua o nga tangata e whingarotia ana, e whingarotia ana, me ngā whingarotia i runga i te Uira me te Kuini. **Explanation of choices:** * **I te waka:** "In a carriage" - *i te* is a locative marker indicating "in" or "on". *Waka* means carriage or vehicle. * **o ratou hoki mai i te Paraipi:** "they traveled back to Paris" - *o ratou* (of them), *hoki mai* (returned), *i te Paraipi* (to Paris). * **i hāngai ai:** "surrounded by" - *i hāngai ai* indicates a state of being surrounded. * **i te aua o nga tangata:** "a mob of people" - *i te aua* (a crowd), *o nga tangata* (of people). * **e whingarotia ana:** "screaming and shouting" - *e... ana* is a continuous passive construction, indicating ongoing action. *Whingarotia* means to scream or shout. Repeating it emphasizes both actions. * **me ngā whingarotia i runga i te Uira me te Kuini:** "threats against the King and Queen" - *me* (and), *ngā whingarotia* (threats), *i runga i* (against/on), *te Uira* (the King), *me te Kuini* (and the Queen). This translation aims to be grammatically correct and conveys the intensity of the original sentence. It uses common Maori vocabulary and structures while preserving the meaning.
Whakarau i te hapi te hapi i huri i te pōhutukawa ki te panoni ngā paraire o te riri. **Explanation of choices & nuances:** * **Whakarau i te hapi:** "Forced by the mob" - "Whakarau" means to force or compel. "I te hapi" means "by the mob/crowd". * **te hapi:** "the mob/crowd" - This is a standard way to refer to a large, forceful group. * **i huri i te pōhutukawa:** "turned the King and Queen" - "I huri" means "turned" or "caused to turn." "Te pōhutukawa" is used here to refer to the King and Queen, it's a respectful and common way to refer to royalty. * **ki te panoni:** "to have (their carriage windows)" - "Ki te panoni" implies causing something to be or to happen. * **ngā paraire o te riri:** "the windows of the carriage" - "Ngā paraire" is windows, and "o te riri" clarifies these are the windows *of the carriage*. "Riri" meaning carriage. **Important Considerations:** * **Formal vs. Informal:** This translation leans toward a more formal/literary style. Depending on the context, a more colloquial phrasing might be used. * **Respect:** When dealing with royalty, Maori often employs respectful language. The choice of "pōhutukawa" is a case of this. * **Verb Conjugation/Grammar:** Maori has a complex verb system. The structure used reflects a slightly more formal construction.

Heचारा ngā punaha mutunga kore a te taura pūmanawa, iro i puta mai i te whakarau purotu i ngā tangata Filipina, e tāwhetu ana te mātāpono mātau o te taura pūmanawa o Aotearoa. **Explanation of choices:** * **Heचारा ngā punaha mutunga kore a te taura pūmanawa:** "Of course, the superprofits derived from..." - This translates to "Certainly, the endless systems of profit..." "Heचारा" conveys the "of course" aspect, and "punaha mutunga kore" is a formal way to express "superprofits". "Taura pūmanawa" means profit. * **iro i puta mai i te whakarau purotu:** "would constitute the basic gains of..." - This translates to "would arise from the exploitative act". "Iro i puta mai i" signifies "arise from" or "result from". "Whakarau purotu" is a direct translation for "exploitation". * **i ngā tangata Filipina:** "the Filipino people" - This is a straightforward translation. * **e tāwhetu ana te mātāpono mātau o te taura pūmanawa o Aotearoa:** "would be the basic gains of U.S. imperialism" - This means "would be the fundamental profit of American imperialism." "Tāwhetu ana" means "to be", "mātāpono mātau" is fundamental profit, and "taura pūmanawa o Aotearoa" translates to "profit of America". **Important Considerations:** * **Formal Language:** This translation uses relatively formal Maori. Depending on the context (e.g., a news report vs. a scholarly paper), adjustments could be made. * **Nuance:** Maori often conveys meaning through context and implied understanding. A literal translation can sometimes miss subtleties. * **"U.S. Imperialism":** Translating "imperialism" directly can be tricky. Using "taura pūmanawa o Aotearoa" in this context aims for clarity without losing the core meaning. It is assumed here that the context allows for this interpretation.

Ko teine pāmimi ngā teine i whakahuahia, me ngā reo aroha me te reo pāmimi. I whakaurunga ngā teine pāmimi i ngā tangata (whānau i te agi), i a pai te mōhio, ka noho tēnei whare i runga i te tangata, ka whai tētahi tūmanako i te tangata i whakakore i teine i te whānau, me ngā takawaheke mangere tamariki. **Explanation of choices and considerations:** * **"Such children"**: "Ko teine pāmimi ngā teine i whakahuahia" - This translates to "These children are the ones being described" or "These children are called". "Pāmimi" is a commonly used term for feral, wild or neglected. * **"feral"**: "pāmimi" - This is the closest equivalent to the English term, carrying the meaning of wild, untamed, and often associated with lack of socialization. * **"wild"**: included within "pāmimi" to provide a fuller understanding of the concept * **"confined by people"**: "I whakaurunga ngā teine pāmimi i ngā tangata" - This translates to "the children were confined by people". * **"usually their own parents"**: "whānau i te agi" - this is a less literal translation of the parents, but is the most natural way to describe when the parents themselves were the ones confining their child. * **"child abandonment"**: "ka noho tēnei whare i runga i te tangata" - this translates to 'this house stood on the person' with connotations of abandonment. * **"rejection of a child's severe intellectual or physical impairment"**: "ka whai tētahi tūmanako i te tangata i whakakore i teine i te whānau, me ngā takawaheke mangere tamariki" - This is a more descriptive translation aiming to convey the reason for abandonment, referencing a child being abandoned because of significant intellectual or physical disabilities. "Takawaheke mangere" refers to disabilities. **Important Note**: Translation is rarely a one-to-one process. I've prioritized creating a sentence that is idiomatic and meaningful in te reo Māori, rather than striving for a literal word-for-word correspondence. The concept of "feral children" is culturally sensitive and historically charged; thus, carefully selecting vocabulary and phrasing is vital to avoid causing offense or misrepresenting the original information. This translation is a good approximation, but it is always best to consult with native speakers for context and nuance.

Ko te titiro ki ngā mea katoa, kia mau tonu tātou, kaore e ngāue ki te mōhio mǎi i te piri atu ki te ara i whakawhanautia e tētahi uri takiwā o tātou i ngā mahi twhakataka i te ahiahi i ngā maunga o te one, mō te tētahi wā. **Explanation of choices made:** * **Ko te titiro ki ngā mea katoa:** "All things considered" - A common and natural way to express this in Maori. * **kia mau tonu tātou:** "we should not be surprised" - "mau tonu" implies persistence and a lack of change in feeling. * **kaore e ngāue ki te mōhio mǎi:** "we should not be surprised" - ngāue (to be surprised/astonished), mōhio mǎi (to know/be aware) * **i te piri atu ki te ara:** "if...in the way/path." "iri" can mean path/route in a more metaphorical sense. * **i whakawhanautia e tētahi uri takiwā o tātou:** "solved by our own ancestors" - "uri takiwā" specifically refers to ancestors. "Whakawhanautia" means to create/develop/solve. * **i te mahi twhakataka:** "protein problem" - "twhakataka" can mean a situation requiring adjustment or a solution; here, it implies a need to address a fundamental issue (like protein). * **i te ahiahi i ngā maunga o te one:** "on the savanna." "Ahiahi" (savanna) is used here for the open grassy plains. "Maunga o te one" (mountains of the plains) is used for the savanna. * **mō te tētahi wā:** "in somewhat the same way that chimps do today" - a temporal indication that the actions are occurring at the present time and the statement refers to a specific time.

Ngā aromātai uri-pūkana ētahi atu e whakauru i te mahi he rere mó o te kapu (e kore ai ana te wai, ā me te rāhu, he rere-whakapūkana) i mua i te hoko, ka mau tonu i te hunkeru ki te hoki ki te hoko i te whānau.
Ko te iti o te atawhai tonu i ngā tuhinga tua onea i ngā taonga o te tinana me te harikoa, te whakapae mai i te iti o te wheako me te hōmekeeping.
Ko te iti rahea o te hākinokino, te hurangi raotia o te manaora. Ka kite tatou i te potentia me te ngāue e ia atu ana i ngā tangata ahau kia kite i te ähiaurangi aroha me te mākoro.
Ka taea e koutou te mutu i te māramatanga. Ko tō tatou wairua pūtake, tō tātou ngākau kururū me te ngākau pūmau anō nei e pā ana i tōna mahi.
Ko te Buddhisma o Taihinga te punaha i kitea i ngā kahui whakaawea a Tāwhirimātea, engari i whakawhanautia i ngā mārara mahayana o te aroha, me ngā mahi o te yoga Ranginiātea. (Note: "Tāwhirimātea" is used here as a culturally sensitive and respectful way to refer to the Buddha, avoiding direct transliteration.)

He nui te akemi i ngā tangata e kaupapa ana i a ratou ngā moa, mō ngā mahi aua i ngā pūkana me te putea. **Explanation of choices:** * **He nui te akemi i ngā tangata:** "Many people don't think about..." (literally, "There is much disbelief in people...") I chose "akemi" (disbelief, lack of consideration) as it captures the sense of not seeing them *as* dinosaurs. * **e kaupapa ana i a ratou ngā moa:** "them as moa" - "e kaupapa ana" meaning "to be considered/regarded" and "ngā moa" being the closest equivalent for "them" when referring to the extinct birds. * **mō ngā mahi aua i ngā pūkana me te putea:** "...because they have feathers and can fly." - "Ngā mahi aua" means "the actions of" or "the characteristics of." "Ngā pūkana me te putea" is a common way to express "feathers and can fly". **Important Considerations:** * **"Dinosaurs" in Maori:** There isn't a direct equivalent for "dinosaur" in traditional Maori. The extinct birds are generally referred to as "moa". * **Nuance:** Maori often expresses ideas with slightly different phrasing than English. I've aimed for a translation that conveys the meaning accurately while sounding natural in Maori. * **Context:** This translation assumes a general context. Depending on the specific context, other phrasing might be more appropriate. * **Variations:** There might be slightly different, equally valid ways to translate this sentence into Maori. This is one good option.

(A slightly more concise/natural option is below) E hūpare ana te tutea करतबā o ngā riri, engari करतबā ngā haere o ngā kuruharu kia kitea mai i te māramatanga o ngā haere o ngā kuruharu, kāore i करतबā te māramatanga o te haere o te kuruharu kia kitea i te 100% māramatanga.
He pai te mea e tino rangatiratira ana ngā kuruhaupo i roto i te waipaa, ā, he mōhio i te mea e tino rite ana te apetona o te waka, ā, e hiki ana te titiro ki ngā rāpare mōhio i ngā ngāhu. **Explanation of choices:** * **He pai te mea e tino rangatiratira ana...**: "Fortunately, it is evident/clear that..." This captures the "fortunately" and the "tend to behave" aspect. "Tino rangatiratira" emphasizes the clear and evident nature. * **ngā kuruhaupo**: "drivers" - "kuruhaupo" is a commonly used and easily understood word for "driver". * **i roto i te waipaa**: "within a reasonably consistent range" - "waipaa" implies a range, consistency or variability. * **ā, he mōhio i te mea e tino rite ana...**: "and, it is known that... reasonably consistent" - conveys the tendency towards consistency. * **te apetona o te waka**: "the pattern/flow of the traffic" - "apetona" signifies a pattern or flow, and "waka" is a general term for vehicle/traffic. * **e hiki ana te titiro ki ngā rāpare mōhio i ngā ngāhu**: "can be roughly represented mathematically" - "titiro" means to look at, and "rāpare mōhio" means 'mathematical patterns'. "Ngā ngāhu" (roughly, in a general way) adds the nuance of "roughly". This is a more natural and idiomatic way to express the concept.

Ngā wahine: He hē karWaitānia kei te koretake ngā wahine e haere ana ki te rere kia kōrero he wahine rangatiratanga, ahakoa te mātāpono o ō ratou whanaungatanga. **Explanation of choices:** * **Ngā wahine:** Women (plural). * **He hē karWaitānia:** It is recommended/considered advisable. "He hē" implies a suggestion or something beneficial. "karWaitānia" is a more formal and respectful way of saying "recommended." * **kei te koretake ngā wahine e haere ana ki te rere:** that women travellers should... This uses the continuous tense "kei te..." "e haere ana ki te rere" literally translates to "are going for travel," effectively capturing the idea of being a traveller. * **kia kōrero:** to say/to speak. * **he wahine rangatiratanga:** that they are married. "Rangatiratanga" is a more polite and respectful way of referring to marital status than a more direct term. It implies a state of being a wife/married woman. * **ahakoa te mātāpono o ō ratou whanaungatanga:** regardless of actual marital status. "Ahakoa" means "although/even if." "Te mātāpono" means the state/status/truth. "Whanaungatanga" is a broad term encompassing family relationships, which is used here to refer to marital status. "ō ratou" means "their."

Ko Te Motu o Honingoa a Horohoro kei te kaupapa o Aotearoa, a ko hea ngā hāngai nui o te ao, me ngā honoakitia nui. **Explanation of choices:** * **Ko Te Motu o Honingoa a Horohoro:** "Hong Kong Island" - literally "The Island of Honingoa" (Horohoro is a common Māori name associated with places). This uses the "ko..." structure to introduce the subject. * **kei te kaupapa o Aotearoa:** "is in the territory of Hong Kong" - This phrase implies a location within the territory. "Kaupapa" can mean foundation, basis, or territory. * **a ko hea ngā hāngai nui o te ao:** "and is the place that many tourists regard as the main focus" - "Hea" is used here to indicate "where" or "the place". "Hāngai nui" means "main focus" or "highlight". "Ngā honoakitia nui" means "many tourists".

Ko te Sundarbans te pariwerte taketake-rahi o te ngahere o te mato i te ao, e rapare ana i te 80 kilometa (50 aria) ki roto i te whenua o te Bangladeshi me te Indian mai i te puree.

Whakaraupae te Nåranga o te Sundarbans i waea o 3,850 km², atu i te tekofua o tekofua o tēnei, i taku tūrua o te wai/pūkana.

Kei te pōuri te hāngai i te tangi mō te takahi i te takahi, ā, kāore he pai kia mahi i mua i te tūmanako. **Translation:** It's very difficult to exchange Falklands currency outside of the islands, so exchange money before you leave the islands. **Explanation of Choices:** * **Kei te pōuri te hāngai...:** "It is very difficult to exchange..." "Pōuri" implies a difficult or unfavorable situation. "Hāngai" is a common verb for exchange, particularly of currency. * **i te tangi mō te takahi i te takahi:** "for the exchange of money." * **ā, kāore he pai kia mahi i mua i te tūmanako:** "So, it is not good to do it before expecting it." This conveys the sense of needing to exchange money in advance to avoid problems later.

Kaore te kai karihi he mea takoti i te mana tangihanga, ā, ka pēhea te mana tangihanga i runga i te miri. **Explanation of choices:** * **Kaore te kai karihi:** "A curry is not..." (used to introduce the concept) * **he mea takoti:** "a thing that is dry" - takoti implies dryness, not just lack of moisture. * **i te mana tangihanga:** "in the state of wetness/liquidity" * **ā:** "and" * **ka pēhea te mana tangihanga:** "how is the state of wetness/liquidity" * **i runga i te miri:** "depending on the amount of liquid" - miri specifically refers to liquid or moisture. This translation attempts to capture the nuance of "dry" and "wet" as states related to liquid content, common in how Maori speakers would describe curry. It's more descriptive than a literal translation, which often sounds unnatural in Maori.

Whakaruaki, ngā pokere ni, ahakoa ngā pokere o te Rangini, e hāngai ana te pakiwaitara hei whakahoki i te pārekete o te hāngai, rānei i te pūkana mai i te kapa i te pōtini. **Explanation of choices:** * **Whakaruaki:** Next, furthermore. * **Ngā pokere ni:** Some saddles. "Ni" indicates "some". * **Ahakoa ngā pokere o te Rangini:** Although English saddles. "Rangini" literally means "sky/heaven", but is commonly used to refer to English things or styles. * **E hāngai ana te pakiwaitara:** Have safety bars that... "Pakiwaitara" is a good general term for a bar or restraint. * **Hei whakahoki i te pārekete o te hāngai:**... to allow the stirrup leather to fall off the saddle. "Whakahoki" means to return/fall off, "pārekete o te hāngai" is a literal translation of stirrup leather. * **rānei i te pūkana mai i te kapa i te pōtini:** ...if pulled backwards by a falling rider. "Pūkana" means to pull, "kapa" means rider, "pōtini" means falling. The "rānei" means "or". Heemi tō reinga i ngā iwi Māori me ngā hononga mōhio, ā, he mea nui hoki ngā tikanga me te kaupapa i ngā taonga tuku iho. **Explanation of choices:** * **Heemi tō reinga i ngā iwi Māori:** "Reindeer husbandry is important to the Sámi people" - I chose "Heemi" meaning to be important, referring to the reindeer husbandry in the context of importance to a specific group. "i ngā iwi Māori" translates to "to the Sámi people", although it’s more descriptive of the group in this context. * **me ngā hononga mōhio:** "and the networks/connections of knowledge" - I used this to capture the "culture surrounding the trade" aspect, emphasizing the interconnectedness of practices and knowledge involved. * **ā, he mea nui hoki:** "and, it is also important" - A simple and direct way to connect the two parts of the sentence. * **ngā tikanga me te kaupapa:** "the practices and the framework" - Captures the idea of the "culture". Tikanga is customs and practices, and kaupapa is a broader concept of principle, framework, or project, which fits the context of the cultural aspect of the trade. * **i ngā taonga tuku iho:** "of the ancestral treasures/heritage" - This captures the "culture surrounding the trade" as relating to the cultural heritage, traditions, and history associated with reindeer husbandry. **Important Note:** Translating concepts across languages, particularly those with vastly different cultural contexts, is always an interpretation. This translation aims to convey the meaning accurately while maintaining a natural flow in Maori. There might be other valid translations, depending on the specific emphasis desired. The concept of a "trade" inherently has different implications within Maori cultural frameworks compared to the English "trade", so careful consideration is important. I've tried to avoid direct equivalents that might not fully capture the nuanced meaning.

He tino pai mua te haere me te tangere mo ngā taonga e hiahia ana ki te haere i te wa mutunga kura. **Explanation of choices:** * **He tino pai mua** - "An increasingly more popular" - This captures the idea of growing popularity. "Tino pai" means "very good" or "excellent," and "mua" implies increasing. * **te haere me te tangere** - "to travel and learn" - A direct translation conveying both actions. * **mo ngā taonga e hiahia ana** - "for those planning" - "Taonga" can mean treasures, valuables, or in this context, people who are valued or those needing something. * **ki te haere i te wa mutunga kura** - "a gap-year" - "Wa mutunga kura" literally means "end of school time," which is the common understanding of a gap year.
Ko tōku whakaaro kei te pai tonu tō kōrero i ngā wahine aroha, e hono ana ki te whai tūunga kore ai i te mātauranga reo Māori, me te noti i te wahanga nei i mua i te whakauru i te whare wananga. **Note:** This translation attempts to capture the nuance of "especially popular" by using "kei te pai tonu" (still good/continues to be good), which implies a strong level of popularity. It also clarifies the purpose of taking a year out as being for the development of Māori language skills. I have also added a reference to "whare wananga" (university) as it is more commonly used in modern contexts.

He wairamohi te taonga i ngā flaxpū, moke whenua, me ngā maakete ngahere, mō tētahi tūroa o ngā ahli raraunga, ngā kanapa whenua, me ngā maunga. **Explanation of choices:** * **He wairamohi te taonga:** This translates to "It is a remarkable treasure/place." "Taonga" is a key word conveying value, beauty, and importance in Maori culture. "Wairamohi" means remarkable or outstanding. * **i ngā flaxpū, moke whenua, me ngā maakete ngahere:** "With flaxlands, forests, and forest communities". This provides a concise list of the plant communities. * **mō tētahi tūroa o ngā ahli raraunga, ngā kanapa whenua, me ngā maunga:** "due to a range of microclimates, differing soils, and varying altitudes." "Ahli raraunga" refers to the microclimates. "Kanapa whenua" means soils. "Maunga" means mountains and encompasses varying altitude. * The sentence structure reflects a more natural Maori flow, using a noun phrase to begin and describing the features that contribute to the richness.

Ko Aotearoa-Rāhina te whenua i takatahi ai te tikanga, i te wā i manaaki ai ngā kōwhai. **Explanation of choices:** * **Ko Aotearoa-Rāhina te whenua i takatahi ai te tikanga:** This translates to "Aotearoa-Rāhina is the land where the concept originated." "Takatahi" means to originate from or come from. * **i te wā i manaaki ai ngā kōwhai:** This translates to "at the time when plum blossoms were favored/cherished". "Manaaki" captures the sense of being the "flower of choice" - implying a favored or beloved status. "Kōwhai" is a more general term referring to plum blossoms, as there isn't a single perfect equivalent for "plum blossom" that fully captures the nuance. **Important note:** Direct, word-for-word translations often don't work in Maori. I've aimed for a translation that conveys the *meaning* and cultural context as naturally as possible. The choice of "kōwhai" is a compromise to preserve a recognizable element of the original concept.
